<p><i><b>American Idol</b></i> is an American singing <a href="/wiki/Reality_competition" class="mw-redirect" title="Reality competition">competition television series</a> created by <a href="/wiki/Simon_Fuller" title="Simon Fuller">Simon Fuller</a>, produced by <a href="/wiki/Fremantle_(company)" title="Fremantle (company)">FremantleMedia North America</a> and <a href="/wiki/19_Entertainment" title="19 Entertainment">19 Entertainment</a>, and distributed by Fremantle North America. It initially aired on <a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">Fox</a> from June 11, 2002, to April 7, 2016, for 15 seasons. Since March 11, 2018, a revival of the series has aired on <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a>.
</p><p>It started as an addition to the <a href="/wiki/Idols_(TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Idols (TV series)"><i>Idols</i> format</a> that was based on <i><a href="/wiki/Pop_Idol" title="Pop Idol">Pop Idol</a></i> from British television, and became one of the most successful shows in the history of <a href="/wiki/Television_in_the_United_States" title="Television in the United States">American television</a>. The concept of the series involves discovering <a href="/wiki/Recording_Artist" class="mw-redirect" title="Recording Artist">recording stars</a> from unsigned singing talents, with the winner determined by American viewers using phones, Internet, and <a href="/wiki/SMS" title="SMS">SMS</a> text voting. The winners of the first seventeen seasons, as chosen by viewers, are <a href="/wiki/Kelly_Clarkson" title="Kelly Clarkson">Kelly Clarkson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ruben_Studdard" title="Ruben Studdard">Ruben Studdard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fantasia_Barrino" title="Fantasia Barrino">Fantasia Barrino</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carrie_Underwood" title="Carrie Underwood">Carrie Underwood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taylor_Hicks" title="Taylor Hicks">Taylor Hicks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordin_Sparks" title="Jordin Sparks">Jordin Sparks</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Cook_(singer)" title="David Cook (singer)">David Cook</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kris_Allen" title="Kris Allen">Kris Allen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lee_DeWyze" title="Lee DeWyze">Lee DeWyze</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotty_McCreery" title="Scotty McCreery">Scotty McCreery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phillip_Phillips" title="Phillip Phillips">Phillip Phillips</a>, <a href="/wiki/Candice_Glover" title="Candice Glover">Candice Glover</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caleb_Johnson" title="Caleb Johnson">Caleb Johnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nick_Fradiani" title="Nick Fradiani">Nick Fradiani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trent_Harmon" title="Trent Harmon">Trent Harmon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maddie_Poppe" title="Maddie Poppe">Maddie Poppe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Laine_Hardy" title="Laine Hardy">Laine Hardy</a> respectively.
</p><p><i>American Idol</i> employs a panel of vocal judges who critique the contestants' performances. The original judges, for the first through eighth seasons, were record producer and music manager <a href="/wiki/Randy_Jackson" title="Randy Jackson">Randy Jackson</a>, singer and choreographer <a href="/wiki/Paula_Abdul" title="Paula Abdul">Paula Abdul</a>, and music executive and manager <a href="/wiki/Simon_Cowell" title="Simon Cowell">Simon Cowell</a>. The judging panel for the last three seasons on Fox consisted of singers <a href="/wiki/Keith_Urban" title="Keith Urban">Keith Urban</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Lopez" title="Jennifer Lopez">Jennifer Lopez</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Harry_Connick,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Harry Connick, Jr.">Harry Connick, Jr.</a><sup id="cite_ref-J-Lo&amp;Connick_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-J-Lo&amp;Connick-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The sixteenth season brought three new judges: singers <a href="/wiki/Lionel_Richie" title="Lionel Richie">Lionel Richie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Katy_Perry" title="Katy Perry">Katy Perry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Luke_Bryan" title="Luke Bryan">Luke Bryan</a>. The first season was hosted by radio personality <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Seacrest" title="Ryan Seacrest">Ryan Seacrest</a> and comedian <a href="/wiki/Brian_Dunkleman" title="Brian Dunkleman">Brian Dunkleman</a>, but Seacrest remained as the sole <a href="/wiki/Master_of_ceremonies" title="Master of ceremonies">master of ceremonies</a> for the rest of the series. The success of <i>American Idol</i> has been described as "unparalleled in broadcasting history".<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> A rival TV executive said the series was "the most impactful show in the history of television".<sup id="cite_ref-carter_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carter-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> It became a recognized springboard for launching the career of many artists as bona fide stars. According to <a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)"><i>Billboard</i> magazine</a>, in its first ten years, "<i>Idol</i> has spawned 345 <i>Billboard</i> chart-toppers and a platoon of pop idols, including Kelly Clarkson, Carrie Underwood, <a href="/wiki/Chris_Daughtry" title="Chris Daughtry">Chris Daughtry</a>, Fantasia, Ruben Studdard, <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Hudson" title="Jennifer Hudson">Jennifer Hudson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clay_Aiken" title="Clay Aiken">Clay Aiken</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adam_Lambert" title="Adam Lambert">Adam Lambert</a>, and Jordin Sparks while remaining a TV ratings juggernaut."<sup id="cite_ref-Billboard2012_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard2012-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For an unprecedented eight consecutive years, from the <a href="/wiki/2003%E2%80%9304_United_States_network_television_schedule" title="2003–04 United States network television schedule">2003–04 television season</a> through the <a href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%9311_United_States_network_television_schedule" title="2010–11 United States network television schedule">2010–11 season</a>, either its performance show or result show was <a href="/wiki/List_of_most_watched_television_broadcasts_in_the_United_States#Most_watched_by_year" title="List of most watched television broadcasts in the United States">ranked number one</a> in U.S. <a href="/wiki/Nielsen_ratings" title="Nielsen ratings">television ratings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ratings_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ratings-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>American Idol</i> was based on the British show <i><a href="/wiki/Pop_Idol" title="Pop Idol">Pop Idol</a></i> created by <a href="/wiki/Simon_Fuller" title="Simon Fuller">Simon Fuller</a>, which was in turn inspired by the New Zealand television singing competition <i><a href="/wiki/Popstars" title="Popstars">Popstars</a></i>. Television producer <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Lythgoe" title="Nigel Lythgoe">Nigel Lythgoe</a> saw a version in Australia and helped bring it over to Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-guardin_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardin-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Fuller was inspired by the idea from <i>Popstars</i> of employing a panel of judges to select singers in audition. He then added other elements, including <a href="/wiki/Televoting" title="Televoting">telephone voting</a> by the viewing public (which at the time was already in use in shows, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Eurovision_Song_Contest" title="Eurovision Song Contest">Eurovision Song Contest</a></i>), the drama of backstories, and real-life soap opera unfolding in real time.<sup id="cite_ref-variety_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-variety-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <i>Pop Idol</i> debuted in Britain in 2001 with Lythgoe as <a href="/wiki/Showrunner" title="Showrunner">showrunner</a>&#8205;&#8212;&#8204;the executive producer and production leader&#8205;&#8212;&#8204;and <a href="/wiki/Simon_Cowell" title="Simon Cowell">Simon Cowell</a> as one of the judges, and was successful with the viewing public.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2001, Fuller, Cowell, and TV producer Simon Jones attempted to sell the <i>Pop Idol</i> format to the United States, but the idea was initially met with poor responses from all the television networks including <a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">Fox</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_–_American_Idol_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_–_American_Idol-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Rupert_Murdoch" title="Rupert Murdoch">Rupert Murdoch</a>, head of <a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">Fox</a>'s <a href="/wiki/News_Corporation_(1980%E2%80%932013)" title="News Corporation (1980–2013)">parent company</a>, was later persuaded to buy the series by his daughter, <a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_Murdoch_(businesswoman)" title="Elisabeth Murdoch (businesswoman)">Elisabeth</a>, who had seen the British show.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_–_American_Idol_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_–_American_Idol-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Although Fox's executives wanted to change the format, Murdoch insisted that it should remain the same as the British one. One change was nevertheless made due to the presence of multiple time zones in the United States that made it impractical for the country to vote in the same time period, an additional half-hour results show was therefore added the day following the performance show.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The show was renamed <i>American Idol: The Search for a Superstar</i> and debuted in the summer of 2002. Cowell was initially offered the job of showrunner, but turned down the offer; Lythgoe then took over that position. Much to the surprise of Cowell and Fox, it became one of the biggest shows of the summer.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watching_TV:_six_decades_of_American_television_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watching_TV:_six_decades_of_American_television-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> With its successful launch in the summer, the show was then moved to January and expanded.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>  The show grew into a phenomenon largely due to its personal engagement with the contestants by prompting the viewers to vote, and the presence of the acid-tongued Cowell as a judge. By 2004, it had become the most-watched show on U.S. television, a position it then held for seven consecutive seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-USA_Today2012_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USA_Today2012-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, after a few years of sharp declining ratings starting in 2012, with rating falls of over 20% each season,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> the fifteenth season would be its last, ending its run in April 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-Idol_Cancellation_(May_2015)_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Idol_Cancellation_(May_2015)-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In May 2017, ABC acquired the rights to the series and the program returned for the 2017–18 television season.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> ABC announced in May 2019 that American Idol will be returning for its eighteenth season.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Judges_and_hosts">Judges and hosts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Judges and hosts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r889293853/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judges">Judges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Judges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The show had originally planned on having four judges following the <i>Pop Idol</i> format; however, only three judges had been found by the time of the audition round in the first season, namely Randy Jackson, Paula Abdul and Simon Cowell.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_–_American_Idol_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_–_American_Idol-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> A fourth judge, radio DJ <a href="/wiki/Stryker_(disc_jockey)" class="mw-redirect" title="Stryker (disc jockey)">Stryker</a>, was originally chosen but he dropped out citing "image concerns".<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In the second season, New York radio personality <a href="/wiki/Angie_Martinez" title="Angie Martinez">Angie Martinez</a> had been hired as a fourth judge but withdrew only after a few days of auditions due to not being comfortable with giving out criticism.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The show decided to continue with the three judges format until the eighth season. All three original judges stayed on the judging panel for eight seasons.
</p><p>In the eighth season, <a href="/wiki/Latin_Grammy_Award" title="Latin Grammy Award">Latin Grammy Award</a>-nominated singer-songwriter and record producer <a href="/wiki/Kara_DioGuardi" title="Kara DioGuardi">Kara DioGuardi</a> was added as a fourth judge. She stayed for two seasons and left the show before the tenth season.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Paula Abdul left the show before the ninth season after failing to agree to terms with the show producers.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Emmy_Award" title="Emmy Award">Emmy Award</a>-winning talk show host <a href="/wiki/Ellen_DeGeneres" title="Ellen DeGeneres">Ellen DeGeneres</a> replaced Paula Abdul for that season, but left after just one season.<sup id="cite_ref-degeneresidol_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-degeneresidol-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> On January 11, 2010, Simon Cowell left the show to pursue introducing the <a href="/wiki/The_X_Factor_(U.S.)" class="mw-redirect" title="The X Factor (U.S.)">American version</a> of his show <i><a href="/wiki/The_X_Factor_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="The X Factor (UK)">The X Factor</a></i> for 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Lopez" title="Jennifer Lopez">Jennifer Lopez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steven_Tyler" title="Steven Tyler">Steven Tyler</a> joined the judging panel in the tenth season,<sup id="cite_ref-newjudges_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newjudges-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> but both left after two seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> They were replaced by three new judges, <a href="/wiki/Mariah_Carey" title="Mariah Carey">Mariah Carey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicki_Minaj" title="Nicki Minaj">Nicki Minaj</a> and <a href="/wiki/Keith_Urban" title="Keith Urban">Keith Urban</a>, who joined Randy Jackson in the twelfth season.<sup id="cite_ref-season_12_judges_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-season_12_judges-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> However both Carey and Minaj left after one season,<sup id="cite_ref-carey_minaj_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carey_minaj-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Randy_Jackson" title="Randy Jackson">Randy Jackson</a> departed the show after twelve seasons as a judge but would return as a mentor.<sup id="cite_ref-I&#39;m_Leaving_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-I&#39;m_Leaving-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-iovine_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iovine-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Urban was the only judge from the tweflth season to return in the thirteenth. He was joined by previous judge Jennifer Lopez and former mentor <a href="/wiki/Harry_Connick,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Harry Connick, Jr.">Harry Connick, Jr.</a><sup id="cite_ref-J-Lo&amp;Connick_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-J-Lo&amp;Connick-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Lopez, Urban and Connick, Jr. all returned as judges for the show's fourteenth and fifteenth seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-judges_season_14_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-judges_season_14-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-deadline-idolcancelled_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deadline-idolcancelled-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Katy_Perry" title="Katy Perry">Katy Perry</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-CNN_1_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN_1-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Luke_Bryan" title="Luke Bryan">Luke Bryan</a><sup id="cite_ref-Bryan_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bryan-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Lionel_Richie" title="Lionel Richie">Lionel Richie</a><sup id="cite_ref-Richie_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richie-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> were the judges on the sixteenth through eighteenth seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Guest judges may occasionally be introduced. In the second season, guest judges such as <a href="/wiki/Lionel_Richie" title="Lionel Richie">Lionel Richie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robin_Gibb" title="Robin Gibb">Robin Gibb</a> were used, and in the third season <a href="/wiki/Donna_Summer" title="Donna Summer">Donna Summer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quentin_Tarantino" title="Quentin Tarantino">Quentin Tarantino</a> and some of the mentors also joined as judges to critique the performances in the final rounds. Guest judges were used in the audition rounds, <a href="/wiki/Gene_Simmons" title="Gene Simmons">Gene Simmons</a> and <a href="/wiki/LL_Cool_J" title="LL Cool J">LL Cool J</a> in the fourth season, <a href="/wiki/Jewel_(singer)" title="Jewel (singer)">Jewel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Olivia_Newton-John" title="Olivia Newton-John">Olivia Newton-John</a> in the sixth season, <a href="/wiki/Shania_Twain" title="Shania Twain">Shania Twain</a> in the eighth season, <a href="/wiki/Neil_Patrick_Harris" title="Neil Patrick Harris">Neil Patrick Harris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avril_Lavigne" title="Avril Lavigne">Avril Lavigne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Katy_Perry" title="Katy Perry">Katy Perry</a> in the ninth season and <a href="/wiki/Adam_Lambert" title="Adam Lambert">Adam Lambert</a> in the fourteenth season.
</p>
<table class="toccolours collapsible collapsed" style="margin:0.5em auto; width:800px;">

<tbody><tr>
<th>Judges timeline
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><div class="timeline-wrapper"><map name="timeline_6cdbc2a2435eaef44cc27de8ec572b4c"></map><img usemap="#timeline_6cdbc2a2435eaef44cc27de8ec572b4c" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/timeline/6cdbc2a2435eaef44cc27de8ec572b4c.png" /></div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hosts">Hosts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Hosts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first season was co-hosted by <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Seacrest" title="Ryan Seacrest">Ryan Seacrest</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brian_Dunkleman" title="Brian Dunkleman">Brian Dunkleman</a> following the format of <i>Pop Idol</i> of using two presenters. Dunkleman quit thereafter,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> making Seacrest the sole <a href="/wiki/Emcee" class="mw-redirect" title="Emcee">emcee</a> of the show starting with the second season. Dunkleman did, however, return in the initial series finale on Fox. Seacrest returned for the sixteenth, seventeenth, and eighteenth seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> On April 8, 2019, <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Bones" title="Bobby Bones">Bobby Bones</a> subbed for Ryan when he was out sick.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Selection_process">Selection process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Selection process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br /><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22American+Idol%22">"American Idol"</a>&#160;–&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22American+Idol%22+-wikipedia">news</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22American+Idol%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22American+Idol%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22American+Idol%22">scholar</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22American+Idol%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">January 2019</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In a series of steps, the show selected the eventual winner out of many tens of thousands of contestants.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contestant_eligibility">Contestant eligibility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Contestant eligibility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The eligible age-range for contestants was fifteen to twenty-eight years old. The initial age limit was sixteen to twenty-four in the first three seasons, but the upper limit was raised to twenty-eight in the fourth season, and the lower limit was reduced to fifteen in the tenth season. The contestants had to be legal U.S. residents, could not have advanced to particular stages of the competition in previous seasons, and must not have held a current recording or talent representation contract by the semi-final stage<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> (in previous years by the audition stage).<sup id="cite_ref-season_4_rules_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-season_4_rules-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin:0 auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:708px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto"><a href="/wiki/File:American_Idol_Denver_auditions_panorama.jpg" class="image" title="Season nine&#39;s Denver audition, at Invesco Field at Mile High."><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/American_Idol_Denver_auditions_panorama.jpg/700px-American_Idol_Denver_auditions_panorama.jpg" decoding="async" width="700" height="167" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/American_Idol_Denver_auditions_panorama.jpg/1050px-American_Idol_Denver_auditions_panorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/American_Idol_Denver_auditions_panorama.jpg/1400px-American_Idol_Denver_auditions_panorama.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4941" data-file-height="1176" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:American_Idol_Denver_auditions_panorama.jpg" title="File:American Idol Denver auditions panorama.jpg"> </a></div>Season nine's Denver audition, at <a href="/wiki/Invesco_Field_at_Mile_High" class="mw-redirect" title="Invesco Field at Mile High">Invesco Field at Mile High</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_auditions">Initial auditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Initial auditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Contestants went through at least three sets of cuts. The first was a brief audition with a few other contestants in front of selectors which may include one of the show's producers. Although auditions can exceed 10,000 in each city, only a few hundred of these made it past the preliminary round of auditions. Successful contestants then sang in front of producers, where more may be cut. Only then can they proceed to audition in front of the judges, which is the only audition stage shown on television.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Those selected by the judges are sent to Hollywood. Between 10–60 people in each city may make it to Hollywood<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hollywood_week_and_Las_Vegas_round">Hollywood week and Las Vegas round</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Hollywood week and Las Vegas round">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Once in Hollywood, the contestants performed individually or in groups in a series of rounds. Until the tenth season, there were usually three rounds of eliminations in Hollywood. In the first round the contestants emerged in groups but performed individually. For the next round, the contestants put themselves in small groups and performed a song together. In the final round, the contestants performed solo with a song of their choice <a href="/wiki/A_cappella" title="A cappella">a cappella</a> or accompanied by a band&#8205;&#8212;&#8204;depending on the season. In the second and third seasons, contestants were also asked to write original lyrics or melody in an additional round after the first round. In the seventh season, the group round was eliminated and contestants may, after a first solo performance and on judges approval, skip a second solo round and move directly to the final Hollywood round. In the twelfth season, the executive producers split up the females and males and chose the members to form the groups in the group round.
</p><p>In the tenth and eleventh seasons, a further round was added in Las Vegas, where the contestants performed in groups based on a theme, followed by one final solo round to determine the semi-finalists. At the end of this stage of the competition, 24 to 36 contestants were selected to move on to the semi-final stage. In the twelfth season the Las Vegas round became a Sudden Death round, where the judges had to choose five guys and five girls each night (four nights) to make the top twenty. In the thirteenth season, the Las Vegas round was eliminated and a new round called "Hollywood or Home" was added, where if the judges were uncertain about some contestants, those contestants were required to perform soon after landing in Los Angeles, and those who failed to impress were sent back home before they reached Hollywood. In the fourteenth season, a Showcase round was added, where the contestants performed at a LA nightclub or auditorium for the judges and a live audience, and these performances determine who makes into the Top 24.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Audience_voting">Audience voting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Audience voting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From the semi-finals onward, the fate of the contestants was decided by public vote. During the contestant's performance as well as the recap at the end, a <a href="/wiki/Toll-free_telephone_number" title="Toll-free telephone number">toll-free telephone number</a> for each contestant was displayed on the screen. For a two-hour period after the episode ends (up to four hours for the finale) in each US time zone, viewers may call or send a text message to their preferred contestant's telephone number, and each call or text message was registered as a vote for that contestant. Viewers were allowed to vote as many times as they can within the two-hour voting window. However, the show reserves the right to discard votes by power dialers.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> One or more of the least popular contestants may be eliminated in successive weeks until a winner emerges. Over 110&#160;million votes were cast in the first season, and by the tenth season the seasonal total had increased to nearly 750 million. Voting via <a href="/wiki/Text_message" class="mw-redirect" title="Text message">text messaging</a> was made available in the second season when <a href="/wiki/AT%26T_Wireless_Services" title="AT&amp;T Wireless Services">AT&amp;T Wireless</a> joined as a sponsor of the show, and 7.5&#160;million text messages were sent to <i>American Idol</i> that season.<sup id="cite_ref-ATT_2003_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATT_2003-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The number of text messages rapidly increased, reaching 178&#160;million texts by the eighth season.<sup id="cite_ref-ATT_2009_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATT_2009-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Online voting was offered for the first time in the tenth season. The votes are counted and verified by Telescope Inc.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Semi-finals">Semi-finals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Semi-finals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the first three seasons, the semi-finalists were split into different groups to perform individually in their respective night. In the first season, there were three groups of ten, with the top three contestants from each group making the finals. In the second and third seasons, there were four groups of eight, and the top two of each selected. These seasons also featured a wildcard round, where contestants who failed to qualify were given another chance. In the first season, only one wildcard contestant was chosen by the judges, giving a total of ten finalists. In the second and third seasons, each of the three judges championed one contestant with the public advancing a fourth into the finals, making 12 finalists in all.
</p><p>From the fourth through seventh and ninth seasons, the twenty-four semi-finalists were divided by gender in order to ensure an equal gender division in the top twelve. The men and women sang separately on consecutive nights, and the bottom two in each groups were eliminated each week until only six of each remained to form the top twelve.
</p><p>The wildcard round returned in the eighth season, wherein there were three groups of twelve, with three contestants moving forward – the highest male, the highest female, and the next highest-placed singer – for each night, and four wildcards were chosen by the judges to produce a final 13. Starting in tenth season, the girls and boys perform on separate nights. In the tenth and eleventh seasons, five of each gender were chosen, and three wildcards were chosen by the judges to form a final 13. In the twelfth season, the top twenty semifinalists were split into gender groups, with five of each gender advancing to form the final 10. In the thirteenth season, there were thirty semifinalists, but only twenty semifinalists (ten for each gender) were chosen by the judges to perform on the live shows, with five in each gender based on the vote and three wildcards chosen by the judges composing the final 13. In the fourteenth season, the top 24 performed at <a href="/wiki/The_Fillmore_Detroit" title="The Fillmore Detroit">The Fillmore Detroit</a>, starting with the 12 males on one night and then the 12 females on the next night. The following week, the same order went for the top 16, with four males eliminated, followed by four females based on the vote. Then, on the first night of finals, a similar sequence from the thirteenth season was used to determine the final 12, with five of each gender based on the vote and two wildcards chosen by the judges. In the fifteenth season, the top 24 performed at <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Saint_Vibiana" title="Cathedral of Saint Vibiana">Cathedral of Saint Vibiana</a> in Los Angeles and were split into two groups of twelve and performed twice, one being a solo performance and one being a duet with a former Idol contestant. In each group, the judges chose 7 contestants to advance to the top 14 where the judges chose 4 to advance to the top 10 and remaining 6 contestants were chosen based on the vote. In the sixteenth season, the top 24 performed at the Academy in the Heart of LA, and the show repeated the process from the previous season. However, instead of Idol alumnus as duet partners, superstar celebrity singers were used as the duet partners. In the seventeenth season, the show repeated the process again. However, instead of a top 24, it's a top 20, the contestants performed at the <a href="/wiki/Wiltern_Theatre" class="mw-redirect" title="Wiltern Theatre">Wiltern Theatre</a> in Los Angeles, and performed solos in one episode, and performed the duets in two episodes.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finals">Finals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Finals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The finals were broadcast in prime time from <a href="/wiki/CBS_Television_City" class="mw-redirect" title="CBS Television City">CBS Television City</a> in Los Angeles, in front of a live studio audience. The finals lasted eight weeks in the first season. From the second to ninth and fourteenth seasons, the finals lasted eleven weeks. The tenth and eleventh seasons lasted for twelve weeks, while the twelfth season lasted for ten weeks. In the thirteenth season, the finals lasted thirteen weeks. The finals lasted seven weeks in the fifteenth season, and six weeks in the sixteenth season. Each finalist performs songs based on a weekly theme which may be a musical genre such as <a href="/wiki/Motown" title="Motown">Motown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Disco" title="Disco">disco</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Big_band" title="Big band">big band</a>, songs by artists such as <a href="/wiki/Michael_Jackson" title="Michael Jackson">Michael Jackson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elvis_Presley" title="Elvis Presley">Elvis Presley</a> or <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a>, or more general themes such as <i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">Billboard</a></i> number-one hits or songs from the contestant's year of birth. Contestants usually worked with a celebrity mentor related to the theme. In the tenth season, <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Iovine" title="Jimmy Iovine">Jimmy Iovine</a> was brought in as a mentor for the season. Initially the contestants sang one song each week, but this was increased to two songs from top four or five onwards, then three songs for the top two or three.
</p><p>The most popular contestants were usually not revealed in the results show. Instead, typically the three contestants (two in later rounds) who received the lowest number of votes was called to the center of the stage. One of these three was usually sent to safety; however the two remaining were not necessarily the bottom two.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The contestant with the fewest votes was then revealed and eliminated from the competition. A montage of the eliminated contestant's time on the show was played and they gave their final performance (from the fourteenth season onward, the montage and the final performance were dropped). However, in the <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_(season_6)" title="American Idol (season 6)">sixth season</a>, during the series' first ever <a href="/wiki/Idol_Gives_Back" title="Idol Gives Back">Idol Gives Back</a> episode, no contestant was eliminated, but on the following week, two were sent home. Moreover, starting in <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_(season_8)" title="American Idol (season 8)">eighth season</a>, the judges may overturn viewers' decision with a "Judges' Save" if they unanimously agreed to. "The save" could only be used once, and only up through the top five. In the eighth to tenth and fourteenth seasons, a double elimination then took place in the week following the activation of the save, but in the eleventh and thirteenth seasons, a regular single elimination took place. The save was not activated in the twelfth season and consequently, a non-elimination took place in the week after its expiration with the votes then carrying over into the following week.
</p><p>The "Fan Save" was introduced in the fourteenth season. During the finals, viewers were given a five-minute window to vote for the contestants in danger of elimination by using their Twitter account to decide which contestant will move on to the next show, starting with the Top 8.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Season_finale">Season finale</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Season finale">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The finale was the two-hour last episode of the season, culminating in revealing the winner. For the first, third through sixth and fourteenth through fifteenth seasons it was broadcast from the <a href="/wiki/Dolby_Theatre" title="Dolby Theatre">Dolby Theatre</a>, which has an audience capacity of approximately 3,400. The second-season finale took place at the <a href="/wiki/Gibson_Amphitheatre" class="mw-redirect" title="Gibson Amphitheatre">Gibson Amphitheatre</a>, which had an audience capacity of over 6,000. In the seventh through thirteenth seasons, the venue was at the <a href="/wiki/Nokia_Theatre_L.A._Live" class="mw-redirect" title="Nokia Theatre L.A. Live">Nokia Theater</a>, which holds an audience of over 7,000. In the sixteenth and seventeenth seasons, the venue did not change from the Finals.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rewards_for_winner_and_finalists">Rewards for winner and finalists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Rewards for winner and finalists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The winner received a record deal with a major label, which may be for up to six albums,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT_02-24-2010_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_02-24-2010-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> and secures a management contract with <i>American Idol</i>-affiliated <a href="/wiki/19_Management" class="mw-redirect" title="19 Management">19 Management</a> (which has the <a href="/wiki/Right_of_first_refusal" title="Right of first refusal">right of first refusal</a> to sign all contestants), as well as various lucrative contracts. All winners prior to the ninth season reportedly earned at least $1&#160;million in their first year as winner.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_02-24-2010_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_02-24-2010-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> At first the contract came with a $250,000-plus advance, but dropped to about $62,500 over the Fox years.<sup id="cite_ref-vty_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vty-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> All the runners-up of the first ten seasons, as well as some of other finalists, had also received record deals with major labels. However, starting in the eleventh season, the runner-up may only be guaranteed a single-only deal.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bertelsmann_Music_Group" title="Bertelsmann Music Group">BMG</a>/<a href="/wiki/Sony_Music_Entertainment" class="mw-redirect" title="Sony Music Entertainment">Sony</a> (first through ninth seasons), <a href="/wiki/Universal_Music_Group" title="Universal Music Group">UMG</a> (tenth through fifteenth seasons), and <a href="/wiki/Disney_Music_Group" title="Disney Music Group">Disney Music Group</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Records" title="Hollywood Records">Hollywood Records</a> (sixteenth season onward) had the right of first refusal to sign contestants for three months after the season's finale. In the fourteenth season, the winner was signed with <a href="/wiki/Big_Machine_Records" title="Big Machine Records">Big Machine Records</a> as the winner, Trent Harmon, was more country.<sup id="cite_ref-vty_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vty-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Prominent music mogul <a href="/wiki/Clive_Davis" title="Clive Davis">Clive Davis</a> also produced some of the selected contestants' albums, such as Kelly Clarkson, Clay Aiken, Fantasia Barrino and Diana DeGarmo. All top 10 (11 in the tenth and twelfth seasons, 5 in the fourteenth season, and 7 in the sixteenth season) finalists earn the privilege of going on a tour, where the participants may each earn a six-figure sum.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Series_overview_and_season_synopses">Series overview and season synopses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Series overview and season synopses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_American_Idol_episodes" title="List of American Idol episodes">List of American Idol episodes</a></div>
<p>Each season premieres with the audition round, taking place in different cities. The audition episodes typically feature a mix of potential finalists, interesting characters and woefully inadequate contestants. Each successful contestant receives a golden ticket to proceed on to the next round in Hollywood. Based on their performances during the Hollywood round (Las Vegas round from the tenth through twelfth seasons), 24 to 36 contestants are selected by the judges to participate in the semifinals. From the semifinals onward the contestants perform their songs live, with the judges making their critiques after each performance. The contestants are voted for by the viewing public, and the outcome of the public votes is then revealed during a results segment. The results segment feature group performances by the contestants as well as guest performers. The Top-three results also features homecoming events for the Top 3 finalists. The season reaches its climax in a two-hour results finale show, where the winner of the season is revealed.
</p><p>With the exception of the first two seasons, the contestants in the semifinals onward perform in front of a studio audience. They perform with a full band in the finals. The current musical director is <a href="/wiki/Kristopher_Pooley" title="Kristopher Pooley">Kris Pooley</a>, who has been with the show since the sixteenth season. In previous seasons, the <i>American Idol</i> band was led by <a href="/wiki/Rickey_Minor" title="Rickey Minor">Rickey Minor</a> (fourth through ninth and thirteenth through fifteenth seasons) and <a href="/wiki/Ray_Chew" title="Ray Chew">Ray Chew</a> (tenth through twelfth seasons). Assistance has also been given by vocal coaches and song arrangers, such as Michael Orland and <a href="/wiki/Debra_Byrd" title="Debra Byrd">Debra Byrd</a> to contestants behind the scene. Starting with the seventh season, contestants may perform with a musical instrument from the Hollywood rounds onward. In later seasons, the contestants were allowed to perform with a musical instrument in the auditions. During the first nine seasons, performances were usually aired live on Tuesday nights, followed by the results shows on Wednesdays, but moved to Wednesdays and Thursdays from the tenth through thirteenth seasons. From the fourteenth season onward, there were no separate results shows. On the fourteenth season, the show aired on Wednesday nights, and on the fifteenth season, Thursday nights. From the sixteenth season onward, it aired on Sundays and Mondays.
</p>
<h3><span id="2002.E2.80.932016:_Fox"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2002–2016:_Fox">2002–2016: Fox</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 2002–2016: Fox">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Season_1">Season 1</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Season 1">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_(season_1)" title="American Idol (season 1)">American Idol (season 1)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kelly_Clarkson_57th_Presidential_Inauguration-cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Kelly_Clarkson_57th_Presidential_Inauguration-cropped.jpg/170px-Kelly_Clarkson_57th_Presidential_Inauguration-cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Kelly_Clarkson_57th_Presidential_Inauguration-cropped.jpg/255px-Kelly_Clarkson_57th_Presidential_Inauguration-cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Kelly_Clarkson_57th_Presidential_Inauguration-cropped.jpg/340px-Kelly_Clarkson_57th_Presidential_Inauguration-cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1556" data-file-height="2211" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kelly_Clarkson_57th_Presidential_Inauguration-cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kelly_Clarkson" title="Kelly Clarkson">Kelly Clarkson</a>, the <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_(season_1)" title="American Idol (season 1)">first season</a> winner</div></div></div>
<p>The first season of <i>American Idol</i> debuted as a summer replacement show in June 2002 on the Fox network. It was co-hosted by Ryan Seacrest and Brian Dunkleman.
</p><p>In the audition rounds, 121 contestants were selected from around 10,000 who attended the auditions. These were cut to 30 for the semifinal, with ten going on to the finals. One semifinalist, Delano Cagnolatti, was disqualified for lying to evade the show's age limit. One of the early favorites, <a href="/wiki/Tamyra_Gray" title="Tamyra Gray">Tamyra Gray</a>, was eliminated at the top four, the first of several such shock eliminations that were to be repeated in later seasons. Christina Christian was hospitalized before the top six result show due to chest pains and palpitations, and she was eliminated while she was in the hospital.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jim_Verraros" title="Jim Verraros">Jim Verraros</a> was the first openly gay contestant on the show; his sexual orientation was revealed on his blog, however it was removed during the competition after a request from the show producers over concerns that it might be unfairly influencing votes.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The final showdown was between <a href="/wiki/Justin_Guarini" title="Justin Guarini">Justin Guarini</a>, one of the early favorites, and <a href="/wiki/Kelly_Clarkson" title="Kelly Clarkson">Kelly Clarkson</a>. Clarkson was not initially thought of as a contender,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> but impressed the judges with some good performances in the final rounds, such as her performance of <a href="/wiki/Aretha_Franklin" title="Aretha Franklin">Aretha Franklin</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/(You_Make_Me_Feel_Like)_A_Natural_Woman" title="(You Make Me Feel Like) A Natural Woman">Natural Woman</a>", and <a href="/wiki/Betty_Hutton" title="Betty Hutton">Betty Hutton</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Stuff_Like_That_There" title="Stuff Like That There">Stuff Like That There</a>", and eventually won the crown on September 4, 2002.
</p><p>In what was to become a tradition, Clarkson performed the coronation song during the finale, and released the song immediately after the season ended. The single, "<a href="/wiki/A_Moment_Like_This" title="A Moment Like This">A Moment Like This</a>", went on to break a 38-year-old record held by <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a> for the biggest leap to number one on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a>. Guarini did not release any song immediately after the show and remains the only runner-up not to do so. Both Clarkson and Guarini made a musical film, <i><a href="/wiki/From_Justin_to_Kelly" title="From Justin to Kelly">From Justin to Kelly</a></i>, which was released in 2003 but was widely panned. Clarkson has since become the most successful <i>Idol</i> contestant internationally,<sup id="cite_ref-bronson_100_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bronson_100-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> with worldwide album sales of more than 23&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Starting September 30, 2006, this season was repackaged as "<i><a href="/wiki/American_Idol_Rewind" title="American Idol Rewind">American Idol Rewind</a></i>" and syndicated directly to stations in the U.S.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Season_2">Season 2</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Season 2">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_(season_2)" title="American Idol (season 2)">American Idol (season 2)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ruben_Studdar_2_cropped.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Ruben_Studdar_2_cropped.JPG/170px-Ruben_Studdar_2_cropped.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Ruben_Studdar_2_cropped.JPG/255px-Ruben_Studdar_2_cropped.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Ruben_Studdar_2_cropped.JPG/340px-Ruben_Studdar_2_cropped.JPG 2x" data-file-width="429" data-file-height="551" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ruben_Studdar_2_cropped.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ruben_Studdard" title="Ruben Studdard">Ruben Studdard</a>, the <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_(season_2)" title="American Idol (season 2)">second season</a> winner</div></div></div>
<p>Following the success of the first season, the second season was moved up to air in January 2003. The number of episodes increased, as did the show's budget and the charge for commercial spots. Dunkleman left the show, leaving Seacrest as the lone host. <a href="/wiki/Kristin_Adams" title="Kristin Adams">Kristin Adams</a> was a correspondent for this season.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Corey_Clark" title="Corey Clark">Corey Clark</a> was disqualified during the finals for having an undisclosed police record; however, he later alleged that he and Paula Abdul had an affair while on the show and that this contributed to his expulsion. Clark also claimed that Abdul gave him preferential treatment on the show due to their affair. The allegations were dismissed by Fox after an independent investigation.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Two semi-finalists were also disqualified that year – Jaered Andrews for an arrest on an assault charge, and <a href="/wiki/Frenchie_Davis" title="Frenchie Davis">Frenchie Davis</a> for having previously modelled for an adult website.<sup id="cite_ref-mtv_–_screening_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mtv_–_screening-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ruben_Studdard" title="Ruben Studdard">Ruben Studdard</a> emerged as the winner, beating <a href="/wiki/Clay_Aiken" title="Clay Aiken">Clay Aiken</a> by a small margin. Out of a total of 24 million votes, Studdard finished just 134,000 votes ahead of Aiken. This slim margin of victory was controversial due to the large number of calls that failed to get through.<sup id="cite_ref-Your_Vote_Doesn&#39;t_Count_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Your_Vote_Doesn&#39;t_Count-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In an interview prior to the fifth season, executive producer <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Lythgoe" title="Nigel Lythgoe">Nigel Lythgoe</a> indicated that Aiken had led the fan voting from the wildcard week onward until the finale.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Both finalists found success after the show, but Aiken out-performed Studdard's coronation song "<a href="/wiki/Flying_Without_Wings#Ruben_Studdard_version" title="Flying Without Wings">Flying Without Wings</a>" with his single release from the show "<a href="/wiki/This_Is_the_Night_(Clay_Aiken_song)" title="This Is the Night (Clay Aiken song)">This Is the Night</a>", as well as in their subsequent album releases. The fourth-place finisher <a href="/wiki/Josh_Gracin" title="Josh Gracin">Josh Gracin</a> also enjoyed some success as a country singer.<sup id="cite_ref-billboard_2012_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-billboard_2012-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Season_3">Season 3</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Season 3">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_(season_3)" title="American Idol (season 3)">American Idol (season 3)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fantasia_Barrino.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Fantasia_Barrino.jpg/170px-Fantasia_Barrino.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Fantasia_Barrino.jpg/255px-Fantasia_Barrino.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Fantasia_Barrino.jpg/340px-Fantasia_Barrino.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1230" data-file-height="1632" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fantasia_Barrino.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fantasia_Barrino" title="Fantasia Barrino">Fantasia Barrino</a>, the <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_(season_3)" title="American Idol (season 3)">third season</a> winner</div></div></div>
<p>The third season premiered on January 19, 2004. One of the most talked-about contestants during the audition process was <a href="/wiki/William_Hung" title="William Hung">William Hung</a> whose off-key rendition of <a href="/wiki/Ricky_Martin" title="Ricky Martin">Ricky Martin</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/She_Bangs" title="She Bangs">She Bangs</a>" received widespread attention. His exposure on <i>Idol</i> landed him a record deal and surprisingly he became the <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_alumni_album_sales" class="mw-redirect" title="American Idol alumni album sales">third best-selling</a> singer from that season.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Much media attention on the season had been focused on the three black singers, <a href="/wiki/Fantasia_Barrino" title="Fantasia Barrino">Fantasia Barrino</a>, <a href="/wiki/LaToya_London" title="LaToya London">LaToya London</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Hudson" title="Jennifer Hudson">Jennifer Hudson</a>, dubbed the Three Divas. All three unexpectedly landed on the bottom three on the top seven result show, with Hudson controversially eliminated.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Elton John, who was one of the mentors that season, called the results of the votes "incredibly racist".<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The prolonged stays of <a href="/wiki/John_Stevens_(singer)" title="John Stevens (singer)">John Stevens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jasmine_Trias" title="Jasmine Trias">Jasmine Trias</a> in the finals, despite negative comments from the judges, had aroused resentment, so much so that John Stevens reportedly received a death threat, which he dismissed as a joke 'blown out of proportion'.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The performance of "<a href="/wiki/Summertime_(George_Gershwin_song)" title="Summertime (George Gershwin song)">Summertime</a>" by Barrino, later known simply as "Fantasia", at Top 8 was widely praised, and Simon Cowell considered it as his favorite <i>Idol</i> moment in the nine seasons he was on the show.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Fantasia and <a href="/wiki/Diana_DeGarmo" title="Diana DeGarmo">Diana DeGarmo</a> were the last two finalists, and Fantasia was crowned as the winner. Fantasia released as her coronation single "<a href="/wiki/I_Believe_(Fantasia_song)" title="I Believe (Fantasia song)">I Believe</a>", a song co-written by the first season finalist Tamyra Gray, and DeGarmo released "<a href="/wiki/Dreams_(Diana_DeGarmo_song)" title="Dreams (Diana DeGarmo song)">Dreams</a>".
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Season_4">Season 4</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Season 4">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_(season_4)" title="American Idol (season 4)">American Idol (season 4)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carrie_underwood14052007_by_cmgoonie9_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Carrie_underwood14052007_by_cmgoonie9_cropped.jpg/170px-Carrie_underwood14052007_by_cmgoonie9_cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Carrie_underwood14052007_by_cmgoonie9_cropped.jpg/255px-Carrie_underwood14052007_by_cmgoonie9_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Carrie_underwood14052007_by_cmgoonie9_cropped.jpg/340px-Carrie_underwood14052007_by_cmgoonie9_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="386" data-file-height="492" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carrie_underwood14052007_by_cmgoonie9_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Carrie_Underwood" title="Carrie Underwood">Carrie Underwood</a>, the <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_(season_4)" title="American Idol (season 4)">fourth season</a> winner</div></div></div>
<p>The fourth season premiered on January 18, 2005; this was the first season of the series to be aired in high definition, although the finale of the third season was also aired in high definition. The number of those attending the auditions by now had increased to over 100,000 from the 10,000 of the first season. The age limit was raised to 28 in this season,<sup id="cite_ref-season_4_rules_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-season_4_rules-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and among those who benefited from this new rule were <a href="/wiki/Constantine_Maroulis" title="Constantine Maroulis">Constantine Maroulis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bo_Bice" title="Bo Bice">Bo Bice</a>, the two rockers of the show.
</p><p>The top 12 finalists originally included <a href="/wiki/Mario_Vazquez" title="Mario Vazquez">Mario Vazquez</a>, but he dropped out citing 'personal reasons'<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> and was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Nikko_Smith" title="Nikko Smith">Nikko Smith</a>. Later, an employee of Freemantle Media, which produces the show, sued the company for wrongful termination, claiming that he was dismissed after complaining about lewd behavior by Vazquez toward him during the show.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the top 11 week, due to a mix-up with the contestants' telephone number, voting was repeated on what was normally the result night, with the result reveal postponed until the following night.
</p><p>In May 2005, <a href="/wiki/Carrie_Underwood" title="Carrie Underwood">Carrie Underwood</a> was announced the winner, with Bice the runner-up. Both Underwood and Bice released the coronation song "<a href="/wiki/Inside_Your_Heaven" title="Inside Your Heaven">Inside Your Heaven</a>", with Underwood's version of the song making her the first country artist ever to debut at number-one on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a> chart.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> As of 2015, Underwood has become the most successful <i>Idol</i> contestant in the U.S.,<sup id="cite_ref-bronson_100_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bronson_100-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> selling 16 million albums in the country,<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> while selling a total of 65 million records worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Season_5">Season 5</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Season 5">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_(season_5)" title="American Idol (season 5)">American Idol (season 5)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taylor_Hicks_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Taylor_Hicks_cropped.jpg/170px-Taylor_Hicks_cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Taylor_Hicks_cropped.jpg/255px-Taylor_Hicks_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Taylor_Hicks_cropped.jpg/340px-Taylor_Hicks_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="438" data-file-height="523" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Taylor_Hicks_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Taylor_Hicks" title="Taylor Hicks">Taylor Hicks</a>, the <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_(season_5)" title="American Idol (season 5)">fifth season</a> winner</div></div></div>
<p>The fifth season began on January 17, 2006. It remains the highest-rated season in the show's run so far. Two of the more prominent contestants during the Hollywood round were the Brittenum twins who were later disqualified for identity theft.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chris_Daughtry" title="Chris Daughtry">Chris Daughtry</a>'s performance of <a href="/wiki/Fuel_(band)" title="Fuel (band)">Fuel</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Hemorrhage_(In_My_Hands)" title="Hemorrhage (In My Hands)">Hemorrhage (In My Hands)</a>" on the show was widely praised and led to an invitation to join the band as Fuel's new lead singer, an invitation he declined.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> His performance of <a href="/wiki/Live_(band)" title="Live (band)">Live</a>'s version of "<a href="/wiki/I_Walk_the_Line" title="I Walk the Line">I Walk the Line</a>" was well received by the judges but later criticized in some quarters for not crediting the arrangement to Live.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> He was eliminated at the top four in a shocking result.
</p><p>On May 30, 2006, <a href="/wiki/Taylor_Hicks" title="Taylor Hicks">Taylor Hicks</a> was named <i>American Idol</i>, with <a href="/wiki/Katharine_McPhee" title="Katharine McPhee">Katharine McPhee</a> the runner-up. "<a href="/wiki/Do_I_Make_You_Proud" title="Do I Make You Proud">Do I Make You Proud</a>" was released as Hicks' first single and McPhee's was "<a href="/wiki/My_Destiny_(Katharine_McPhee_song)" title="My Destiny (Katharine McPhee song)">My Destiny</a>".
</p><p>Despite being eliminated earlier in the season, Chris Daughtry (as lead of the band <a href="/wiki/Daughtry_(band)" title="Daughtry (band)">Daughtry</a>) became the most successful recording artist from this season.<sup id="cite_ref-usatoday_2014_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usatoday_2014-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Other contestants, such as Hicks, McPhee, <a href="/wiki/Bucky_Covington" title="Bucky Covington">Bucky Covington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mandisa" title="Mandisa">Mandisa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kellie_Pickler" title="Kellie Pickler">Kellie Pickler</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Elliott_Yamin" title="Elliott Yamin">Elliott Yamin</a> have had varying levels of success.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Season_6">Season 6</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Season 6">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_(season_6)" title="American Idol (season 6)">American Idol (season 6)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jordin_Sparks_in_Parade_cropped.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Jordin_Sparks_in_Parade_cropped.JPG/170px-Jordin_Sparks_in_Parade_cropped.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Jordin_Sparks_in_Parade_cropped.JPG/255px-Jordin_Sparks_in_Parade_cropped.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Jordin_Sparks_in_Parade_cropped.JPG/340px-Jordin_Sparks_in_Parade_cropped.JPG 2x" data-file-width="483" data-file-height="586" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jordin_Sparks_in_Parade_cropped.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jordin_Sparks" title="Jordin Sparks">Jordin Sparks</a>, the <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_(season_6)" title="American Idol (season 6)">sixth season</a> winner</div></div></div>
<p>The sixth season began on Tuesday, January 16, 2007. The premiere drew a massive audience of 37.3&#160;million viewers, peaking in the last half hour with more than 41&#160;million viewers.<sup id="cite_ref-death_star_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-death_star-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Teenager <a href="/wiki/Sanjaya_Malakar" title="Sanjaya Malakar">Sanjaya Malakar</a> was the season's most talked-about contestant for his unusual hairdo,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> and for managing to survive elimination for many weeks due in part to the weblog <a href="/wiki/Vote_for_the_Worst" title="Vote for the Worst">Vote for the Worst</a> and satellite radio personality <a href="/wiki/Howard_Stern" title="Howard Stern">Howard Stern</a>, who both encouraged fans to vote for him. However, on the Top 7 results, Sanjaya was voted off.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This season saw the first <i>Idol Gives Back</i> telethon-inspired event, which raised more than $76&#160;million in corporate and viewer donations.<sup id="cite_ref-idol_gives_back_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-idol_gives_back-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> No contestant was eliminated that week, but two (<a href="/wiki/Phil_Stacey" title="Phil Stacey">Phil Stacey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chris_Richardson" title="Chris Richardson">Chris Richardson</a>) were eliminated the next.
</p><p>In the May 23 season finale, <a href="/wiki/Jordin_Sparks" title="Jordin Sparks">Jordin Sparks</a> was declared the winner with the runner-up being <a href="/wiki/Blake_Lewis" title="Blake Lewis">Blake Lewis</a>. Sparks has had some success as a recording artist post-<i>Idol</i>.
</p><p>This season also saw the launch of the <i>American Idol</i> Songwriter contest which allows fans to vote for the "coronation song". Thousands of recordings of original songs were submitted by songwriters, and 20 entries selected for the public vote. The winning song, "<a href="/wiki/This_Is_My_Now" title="This Is My Now">This Is My Now</a>", was performed by both finalists during the finale and released by Sparks on May 24, 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Season_7">Season 7</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Season 7">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_(season_7)" title="American Idol (season 7)">American Idol (season 7)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Cook_Toads_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/David_Cook_Toads_cropped.jpg/170px-David_Cook_Toads_cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/David_Cook_Toads_cropped.jpg/255px-David_Cook_Toads_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/David_Cook_Toads_cropped.jpg/340px-David_Cook_Toads_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="454" data-file-height="539" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Cook_Toads_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/David_Cook_(singer)" title="David Cook (singer)">David Cook</a>, the <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_(season_7)" title="American Idol (season 7)">seventh season</a> winner</div></div></div>
<p>The seventh season premiered on January 15, 2008, for a two-day, four-hour premiere. The media focused on the professional status of the seventh season contestants, the so-called 'ringers',<sup id="cite_ref-ringers_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ringers-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> many of whom, including <a href="/wiki/Kristy_Lee_Cook" title="Kristy Lee Cook">Kristy Lee Cook</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brooke_White" title="Brooke White">Brooke White</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Johns_(singer)" title="Michael Johns (singer)">Michael Johns</a>, and in particular <a href="/wiki/Carly_Smithson" title="Carly Smithson">Carly Smithson</a>, had prior recording contracts.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Contestant <a href="/wiki/David_Hernandez_(singer)" title="David Hernandez (singer)">David Hernandez</a> also attracted some attention due to his past employment as a stripper.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For the finals, <i>American Idol</i> debuted a new state-of-the-art set and stage on March 11, 2008, along with a new on-air look. <a href="/wiki/David_Cook_(singer)" title="David Cook (singer)">David Cook</a>'s performance of "<a href="/wiki/Billie_Jean" title="Billie Jean">Billie Jean</a>" on top-ten night was lauded by the judges, but provoked controversy when they apparently mistook the <a href="/wiki/Chris_Cornell" title="Chris Cornell">Chris Cornell</a> arrangement to be David Cook's own even though the performance was introduced as Cornell's version. Cornell himself said he was 'flattered' and praised David Cook's performance.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> David Cook was taken to the hospital after the top-nine performance show due to heart palpitations and high blood pressure.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/David_Archuleta" title="David Archuleta">David Archuleta</a>'s performance of John Lennon's "<a href="/wiki/Imagine_(John_Lennon_song)" title="Imagine (John Lennon song)">Imagine</a>" was considered by many as one of the best of the season. Jennifer Lopez, who was brought in as a judge in the tenth season, called it a beautiful song-moment that she will never forget.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jason_Castro_(singer)" title="Jason Castro (singer)">Jason Castro</a>'s semi-final performance of "<a href="/wiki/Hallelujah_(Leonard_Cohen_song)" title="Hallelujah (Leonard Cohen song)">Hallelujah</a>" also received considerable attention, and it propelled <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Buckley" title="Jeff Buckley">Jeff Buckley</a>'s version of the song to the top of the <i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">Billboard</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Hot_Digital_Songs" class="mw-redirect" title="Hot Digital Songs">digital song</a> chart.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>  This was the first season in which contestants' recordings were released onto <a href="/wiki/ITunes" title="ITunes">iTunes</a> after their performances, and although sales information was not released so as not to prejudice the contest, leaked information indicated that contestants' songs frequently reached the top of iTunes sales charts.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Idol Gives Back</i> returned on April 9, 2008, and raised $64&#160;million for charity.<sup id="cite_ref-idol_gives_back_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-idol_gives_back-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The finalists were Cook and Archuleta. David Cook was announced the winner on May 21, 2008, the first rocker to win the show. Both Cook and Archuleta had some success as recording artists with both selling over a million albums in the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-usatoday_2014_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usatoday_2014-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>American Idol</i> Songwriter contest was also held this season. From ten of the most popular submissions, each of the final two contestants chose a song to perform, although neither of their selections was used as the "coronation song". The winning song, "<a href="/wiki/The_Time_of_My_Life_(David_Cook_song)" title="The Time of My Life (David Cook song)">The Time of My Life</a>", was recorded by David Cook and released on May 22, 2008.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Season_8">Season 8</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Season 8">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
