<p><i>American Idol</i> premiered in June 2002 and became the surprise summer hit show of 2002. The first show drew 9.9&#160;million viewers, giving Fox the best viewing figure for the 8.30&#160;pm spot in over a year.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> The audience steadily grew, and by finale night, the audience had averaged 23&#160;million, with more than 40&#160;million watching some part of that show.<sup id="cite_ref-NY_Times_–_September_6,_2002_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NY_Times_–_September_6,_2002-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> That episode was placed third amongst all age groups, but more importantly it led in the in <a href="/wiki/Key_demographic" title="Key demographic">18–49 demographic</a>, the age group most valued by advertisers.<sup id="cite_ref-Watching_TV:_six_decades_of_American_television_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watching_TV:_six_decades_of_American_television-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The growth continued into the next season, starting with a season premiere of 26.5 million.<sup id="cite_ref-ratings_rat_race_135-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ratings_rat_race-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> The season attracted an average of 21.7&#160;million viewers, and was placed second overall amongst the 18–49 age group.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> The finale night when Ruben Studdard won over Clay Aiken was also the highest-rated ever <i>American Idol</i> episode at 38.1&#160;million for the final hour.<sup id="cite_ref-realitytvworld_5/24/2007_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-realitytvworld_5/24/2007-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> By the third season, the show had become the top show in the 18–49 demographic<sup id="cite_ref-realitytvworld_5/24/2007_191-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-realitytvworld_5/24/2007-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> a position it has held for all subsequent years up to and including the tenth season, and its competition stages ranked first in the nationwide overall ratings. By the fourth season, <i>American Idol</i> had become the most watched series amongst all viewers on American TV for the first time, with an average viewership of 26.8&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-MSNBC_January_10,_2007_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MSNBC_January_10,_2007-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> The show reached its peak in the fifth season with numbers averaging 30.6&#160;million per episode, and this season remains the highest-rated of the series.<sup id="cite_ref-MSNBC_January_10,_2007_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MSNBC_January_10,_2007-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The sixth season premiered with the series' highest-rated debut episode and a few of its succeeding episodes rank among the most watched episodes of <i>American Idol.</i> During this time, many television executives begun to regard the show as a programming force unlike any seen before,<sup id="cite_ref-carter_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carter-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> as its consistent dominance of up to two hours two or three nights a week exceeded the 30- or 60-minute reach of previous hits such as NBC's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cosby_Show" title="The Cosby Show">The Cosby Show</a></i>. The show was dubbed "the <a href="/wiki/Death_Star" title="Death Star">Death Star</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-death_star_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-death_star-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> and competing networks often rearranged their schedules in order to minimize losses.<sup id="cite_ref-carter_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carter-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> However, the sixth season also showed a steady decline in viewership over the course of the season. The season finale saw a drop in ratings of 16% from the previous year. The sixth season was the first season wherein the average results show rated higher than the competition stages (unlike in the previous seasons), and became the second-highest-rated of the series after the preceding season.<sup id="cite_ref-realitytvworld_5/24/2007_191-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-realitytvworld_5/24/2007-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The loss of viewers continued into the seventh season. The premiere was down 11% among total viewers,<sup id="cite_ref-ratings_rat_race_135-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ratings_rat_race-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> and the results show in which Kristy Lee Cook was eliminated delivered its lowest-rated Wednesday show among the 18–34 demo since the first season in 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> However, the ratings rebounded for the seventh-season finale with the excitement over the battle of the Davids, and improved over the sixth season as the series' third most watched finale. The strong finish of season seven also helped Fox become the most watched TV network in the country for the first time since its inception, a first ever in <a href="/wiki/American_television" class="mw-redirect" title="American television">American television</a> history for a non-<a href="/wiki/Big_Three_television_networks" title="Big Three television networks">Big Three</a> major broadcast network.<sup id="cite_ref-Fox_wins_TV_season_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fox_wins_TV_season-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> Overall ratings for the season were down 10% from the sixth season,<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> which is in line with the fall in viewership across all networks due in part to the <a href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%932008_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike" class="mw-redirect" title="2007–2008 Writers Guild of America strike">2007–2008 Writers Guild of America strike</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The declining trend however continued into the eighth season, as total viewers numbers fell by 5–10% for early episodes compared to the seventh season,<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> and by 9% for the finale.<sup id="cite_ref-TV_by_the_numbers_MAY_27,_2010_136-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TV_by_the_numbers_MAY_27,_2010-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> In the ninth season, <i>Idol</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> six-year extended streak of perfection in the ratings was broken, when NBC's coverage of the <a href="/wiki/2010_Winter_Olympics" title="2010 Winter Olympics">2010 Winter Olympics</a> on February 17 beat <i>Idol</i> in the same time slot with 30.1&#160;million viewers over <i>Idol'</i>s 18.4&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>  Nevertheless, <i>American Idol</i> overall finished its ninth season as the most watched TV series for the sixth year running, breaking the previous record of five consecutive seasons achieved by CBS' <i><a href="/wiki/All_in_the_Family" title="All in the Family">All in the Family</a></i> and NBC's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cosby_Show" title="The Cosby Show">The Cosby Show</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-&#39;Idol&#39;_is_TV&#39;s_No._1_show_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-&#39;Idol&#39;_is_TV&#39;s_No._1_show-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the tenth season, the total viewer numbers for the first week of shows fell 12–13%, and by up to 23% in the 18–49 demo compared to the ninth season.<sup id="cite_ref-TVbythenumber_01-25-2011_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TVbythenumber_01-25-2011-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Later episodes, however, retained viewers better, and the season ended on a high with a significant increase in viewership for the finale – up 12% for the adults 18–49 demographic and a 21% increase in total viewers from the ninth season finale.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> While the overall viewer number has increased this season, its viewer demographics have continued to age year on year – the median age this season was 47.2 compared to a median age of 32.1 in its first season.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> The demographics also became "whiter" over time and less diverse.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, in the 2010–11 television season, Fox maintained its lead on over other networks with its seventh consecutive season of victory overall in the 18–49 demographic ratings in the United States.
</p><p>The eleventh season, however, suffered a steep drop in ratings, a drop attributed by some to the arrival of new shows such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Voice_(U.S.)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Voice (U.S.)">The Voice</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_X_Factor_(U.S._TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="The X Factor (U.S. TV series)">The X Factor</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> The ratings for the first two episodes of the eleventh season fell 16–21% in overall viewer numbers and 24–27% in the 18/49 demo,<sup id="cite_ref-week1_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-week1-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> while the season finale fell 27% in total viewer number and 30% in the 18–49 demo.<sup id="cite_ref-may2012_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-may2012-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> The average viewership for the season fell below 20 million viewers the first time since 2003, a drop of 23% in total viewers and 30% in the 18/49 demo. For the first time in eight years, <i>American Idol</i> lost the leading position in both the total viewers number and the 18/49 demo, coming in second to <i><a href="/wiki/NBC_Sunday_Night_Football" title="NBC Sunday Night Football">NBC Sunday Night Football</a></i>, although the strengths of <i>Idol</i> in its second year in the Wednesday-Thursday primetime slots helped Fox achieve the longest period of 18–49 demographic victory in the Nielsen ratings, standing at 8 straight years from 2004 to 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-deadline2012_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deadline2012-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The loss of viewers continued into the twelfth season, which saw the show hitting a number of series low in the 18–49 demo.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> The finale had 7.2 million fewer viewers than the previous season, and saw a drop of 44% in the 18–49 demo.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> The season viewers averaged at 13.3 million, a drop of 24% from the previous season.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> The thirteenth season suffered a huge decline in the 18–49 demographic, a drop of 28% from the twelfth season, and <i>American Idol</i> lost its Top 10 position in the Nielsen ratings by the end of the 2013–14 television season for the first time since its entry to the rankings in 2003 as a result, and never regained its Top 10 position by the series' end in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-judges_season_14_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-judges_season_14-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The continuing decline influenced further changes for the fourteenth season, including the loss of <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a> as the show's major sponsor, and a decision to only broadcast one, two-hour show per week during the top 12 rounds (with results from the previous week integrated into the performance show, rather than having a separate results show).<sup id="cite_ref-variety-idolsing_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-variety-idolsing-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-deadline-idolcancelled_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deadline-idolcancelled-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bosglobe-idol2015_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bosglobe-idol2015-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> On May 11, 2015, prior to the fourteenth-season finale, Fox announced that the fifteenth season of <i>American Idol</i> would be its last.<sup id="cite_ref-Idol_Cancellation_(May_2015)_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Idol_Cancellation_(May_2015)-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Despite these changes, the show's ratings would decline more sharply. The fourteenth-season finale was the lowest-rated finale ever, with an average of only 8.03 million viewers watching the finale. The show's ratings, however, rebounded in its final season, and ended its run in 2016 as Fox's first ever program to conclude its run without dropping from the Nielsen Top 30 most watched television shows in each of its seasons.
</p><p>For the revived series on ABC, the ratings were lower but remained stable compared to previous seasons on Fox. The finale of the seventeenth season was slightly more watched than the sixteenth.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_reception">Critical reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Critical reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Early reviews were mixed in their assessment. Ken Tucker of <i><a href="/wiki/Entertainment_Weekly" title="Entertainment Weekly">Entertainment Weekly</a></i> considered that "As TV, <i>American Idol</i> is crazily entertaining; as music, it's dust-mote inconsequential".<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> Others, however, thought that "the most striking aspect of the series was the genuine talent it revealed".<sup id="cite_ref-Watching_TV:_six_decades_of_American_television_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watching_TV:_six_decades_of_American_television-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> It was also described as a "sadistic musical bake-off",<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> and "a romp in humiliation".<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> Other aspects of the show have attracted criticisms. The product placement in the show in particular was noted,<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> and some critics were harsh about what they perceived as its blatant commercial calculations – Karla Peterson of <i><a href="/wiki/The_San_Diego_Union-Tribune" title="The San Diego Union-Tribune">The San Diego Union-Tribune</a></i> charged that <i>American Idol</i> is "a conniving multimedia monster" that has "absorbed the sin of our debauched culture and spit them out in a lump of reconstituted evil".<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> The decision to send the first season winner to sing the national anthem at the Lincoln Memorial on the first anniversary of the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> in 2002 was also poorly received by many. <a href="/wiki/Lisa_de_Moraes" title="Lisa de Moraes">Lisa de Moraes</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> noted sarcastically that "The terrorists have won" and, with a sideswipe at the show's commercialism and voting process, that the decision as to who "gets to turn this important site into just another cog in the 'Great <i>American Idol</i> Marketing Mandala' is in the hands of the millions of girls who have made <i>American Idol</i> a hit. Them and a handful of phone-redialer geeks who have been clocking up to 10,000 calls each week for their contestant of choice (but who, according to Fox, are in absolutely no way skewing the outcome)."<sup id="cite_ref-lisa_de_moraes_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lisa_de_moraes-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of the later writers about the show were more positive, Michael Slezak, again of <i>Entertainment Weekly</i>, thought that "for all its bloated, synthetic, product-shilling, money-making trappings, <i>Idol</i> provides a once-a-year chance for the average American to combat the evils of today's music business."<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> Singer <a href="/wiki/Sheryl_Crow" title="Sheryl Crow">Sheryl Crow</a>, who was later to act as a mentor on the show, however took the view that the show "undermines art in every way and promotes commercialism".<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> Pop music critic <a href="/wiki/Ann_Powers" title="Ann Powers">Ann Powers</a> nevertheless suggested that Idol has "reshaped the American songbook", "led us toward a new way of viewing ourselves in relationship to mainstream popular culture", and connects "the classic Hollywood dream to the multicentered popular culture of the future."<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Others focused on the personalities in the show; Ramin Setoodeh of <i><a href="/wiki/Newsweek" title="Newsweek">Newsweek</a></i> accused judge Simon Cowell's cruel critiques in the show of helping to establish in the wider world a culture of meanness, that "Simon Cowell has dragged the rest of us in the mud with him."<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> Some such as singer <a href="/wiki/John_Mayer" title="John Mayer">John Mayer</a> disparaged the contestants, suggesting that those who appeared on <i>Idol</i> are not real artists with self-respect.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some in the entertainment industry were critical of the star-making aspect of the show. <a href="/wiki/Usher_(entertainer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Usher (entertainer)">Usher</a>, a mentor on the show, bemoaning the loss of the "true art form of music", thought that shows like <i>American Idol</i> made it seem "so easy that everyone can do it, and that it can happen overnight", and that "television is a lie".<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> Musician <a href="/wiki/Michael_Feinstein" title="Michael Feinstein">Michael Feinstein</a>, while acknowledging that the show had uncovered promising performers, said that <i>American Idol</i> "isn't really about music. It's about all the bad aspects of the music business – the arrogance of commerce, this sense of 'I know what will make this person a star; artists themselves don't know.' "<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> That <i>American Idol</i> is seen to be a fast track to success for its contestants has been a cause of resentment for some in the industry. <a href="/wiki/LeAnn_Rimes" title="LeAnn Rimes">LeAnn Rimes</a>, commenting on Carrie Underwood winning Best Female Artist in Country Music Awards over <a href="/wiki/Faith_Hill" title="Faith Hill">Faith Hill</a> in 2006, said that "Carrie has not paid her dues long enough to fully deserve that award".<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> It is a common theme that has been echoed by many others. <a href="/wiki/Elton_John" title="Elton John">Elton John</a>, who had appeared as a mentor in the show but turned down an offer to be a judge on <i>American Idol</i>, commenting on talent shows in general, said that "there have been some good acts but the only way to sustain a career is to pay your dues in small clubs".<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup>  <i>American Idol</i> revolutionized American pop culture and the pop idol process and has provided an opportunity for many to bypass the small club scene and allow a much larger audience to participate in and select the next potential chart topping performer.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The success of the show's alumni, however, has led to a more positive assessment of the show, and the show was described as having "proven it has a valid way to pick talent and a proven way to sell records".<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> While the industry is divided on the show success, its impact is felt particularly strongly in the country music format.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> According to a <a href="/wiki/Country_Music_Television" class="mw-redirect" title="Country Music Television">CMT</a> exec, reflecting on the success of Idol alumni in the country genre, "if you want to try and get famous fast by going to a cattle call audition on TV, Idol reasonably remains the first choice for anyone", and that country music and Idol "go together well".<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>American Idol</i> was nominated for the Emmy's <a href="/wiki/Primetime_Emmy_Award_for_Outstanding_Reality-Competition_Program" class="mw-redirect" title="Primetime Emmy Award for Outstanding Reality-Competition Program">Outstanding Reality Competition Program</a> for nine years but never won.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> Director Bruce Gower won a <a href="/wiki/Primetime_Emmy_Award" title="Primetime Emmy Award">Primetime Emmy Award</a> for <a href="/wiki/61st_Primetime_Emmy_Awards#Directing" title="61st Primetime Emmy Awards">Outstanding Directing For A Variety, Music Or Comedy Series</a> in 2009, and the show won a <a href="/wiki/Creative_Arts_Emmy_Award" title="Creative Arts Emmy Award">Creative Arts Emmys</a> each in 2007 and 2008, three in 2009, and two in 2011, as well as a <a href="/wiki/Emmy_Award#Other_Emmys" title="Emmy Award">Governor's Award</a> in 2007 for its Idol Gives Back edition. It won the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Choice_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="People&#39;s Choice Award">People's Choice Award</a>, which honors the popular culture of the previous year as voted by the public, for favorite competition/reality show in 2005, 2006, 2007, 2010, 2011 and 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> It won the first <a href="/wiki/Critics%27_Choice_Television_Award" title="Critics&#39; Choice Television Award">Critics' Choice Television Award</a> in 2011 for Best Reality Competition.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013, <a href="/wiki/TV_Guide" title="TV Guide">TV Guide</a> ranked the series No. 48 on its list of the 60 Best Series of All Time.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Geographical.2C_ethnic.2C_and_gender_bias"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographical,_ethnic,_and_gender_bias">Geographical, ethnic, and gender bias</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Geographical, ethnic, and gender bias">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Throughout the series, twelve of the seventeen <i>Idol</i> winners, including its first five, had come from the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">Southern United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MSNBC10_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MSNBC10-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> A large number of other finalists during the series' run have also hailed from the American South, including Clay Aiken, Kellie Pickler, and Chris Daughtry,<sup id="cite_ref-MSNBC10_233-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MSNBC10-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> who are all from North Carolina. In 2012, an analysis of the 131 contestants who have appeared in the finals of all seasons of the show up to that point found that 48% have some connection to the Southern United States.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The show itself was popular in the Southern United States, with households in the Southeastern United States 10% more likely to watch <i>American Idol</i> during the eighth season in 2009, and those in the East Central region, such as <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a>, were 16 percent more likely to tune into the series.<sup id="cite_ref-MSNBC10_233-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MSNBC10-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> Data from <a href="/wiki/Nielsen_SoundScan" title="Nielsen SoundScan">Nielsen SoundScan</a>, a music-sales tracking service, showed that of the 47&#160;million CDs sold by <i>Idol</i> contestants through January 2010, 85 percent were by contestants with ties to the American South.<sup id="cite_ref-MSNBC10_233-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MSNBC10-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Theories given for the success of Southerners on <i>Idol</i> have been: more versatility with musical genres, as the Southern U.S. is home to several music genre scenes; not having as many opportunities to break into the pop music business; text-voting due to the South having the highest percentage of cell-phone only households; and the strong heritage of music and singing, which is notable in the <a href="/wiki/Bible_Belt" title="Bible Belt">Bible Belt</a>, where it is in church that many people get their start in public singing.<sup id="cite_ref-MSNBC10_233-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MSNBC10-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> Others also suggest that the Southern character of these contestants appeal to the South, as well as local pride.<sup id="cite_ref-MSNBC04_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MSNBC04-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> According to fifth season winner Taylor Hicks, who is from the state of <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, "People in the South have a lot of pride ... So, they're adamant about supporting the contestants who do well from their state or region."<sup id="cite_ref-MSNBC10_233-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MSNBC10-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For five consecutive seasons, starting in the seventh season, the title was given to a white male who plays the guitar – a trend that <i>Idol</i> pundits call the "White guy with guitar" or "WGWG" factor.<sup id="cite_ref-Expert_opinion_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Expert_opinion-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> Just hours before the eleventh-season finale, where Phillip Phillips was named the winner, Richard Rushfield, author of the book <i>American Idol: The Untold Story</i>, said, "You have this alliance between young girls and grandmas and they see it, not necessarily as a contest to create a pop star competing on the contemporary radio, but as .... who's the nicest guy in a popularity contest", he says, "And that has led to this dynasty of four, and possibly now five, consecutive, affable, very nice, good-looking white boys."<sup id="cite_ref-Expert_opinion_238-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Expert_opinion-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy">Controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_controversies" title="American Idol controversies">American Idol controversies</a></div>
<p>The show had been criticized in earlier seasons over the onerous contract contestants had to sign that gave excessive control to <a href="/wiki/19_Entertainment" title="19 Entertainment">19 Entertainment</a> over their future career,<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> and handed a large part of their future earnings to the management.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Individual contestants have generated controversy in this competition for their past actions,<sup id="cite_ref-mtv_–_screening_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mtv_–_screening-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> or for being 'ringers' planted by the producers.<sup id="cite_ref-ringers_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ringers-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> A number of contestants had been disqualified for various reasons, such as for having an existing contract or undisclosed criminal records, although the show had been accused of double standard for disqualifying some but not others.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Voting results have been a consistent source of controversy. The mechanism of voting had also aroused considerable criticisms, most notably in the second season when Ruben Studdard beat Clay Aiken in a close vote,<sup id="cite_ref-Your_Vote_Doesn&#39;t_Count_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Your_Vote_Doesn&#39;t_Count-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> and in the eighth season, when the massive increase in text votes<sup id="cite_ref-ATT_2009_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATT_2009-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> fueled the texting controversy.<sup id="cite_ref-textgate_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-textgate-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Concerns about power voting have been expressed from the very first season.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> Since 2004, votes also have been affected to a limited degree by online communities such as <a href="/wiki/DialIdol" title="DialIdol">DialIdol</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vote_for_the_Worst" title="Vote for the Worst">Vote for the Worst</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_impact">Cultural impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Cultural impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Television">Television</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Television">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The enormous success of the show and the revenue it generated was transformative for the Fox Broadcasting Company. <i>American Idol</i> and fellow competing shows <i><a href="/wiki/Survivor_(U.S._TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Survivor (U.S. TV series)">Survivor</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Who_Wants_to_Be_a_Millionaire_(U.S._game_show)" class="mw-redirect" title="Who Wants to Be a Millionaire (U.S. game show)">Who Wants to Be a Millionaire</a></i> were altogether credited for expanding <a href="/wiki/Reality_television" title="Reality television">reality television</a> programming in the United States in the 1990s and 2000s, and <i>Idol</i> became the most watched non-scripted primetime television series for almost a decade, from 2003 to 2012, breaking records on U.S. television (dominated by <a href="/wiki/Drama" title="Drama">drama</a> shows and <a href="/wiki/Sitcoms" class="mw-redirect" title="Sitcoms">sitcoms</a> in the preceding decades).<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The show pushed Fox to become the number one U.S. TV network amongst adults 18–49,<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> the key demographic coveted by advertisers, for an unprecedented eight consecutive years by 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> Its success also helped lift the ratings of other shows that were scheduled around it such as <i><a href="/wiki/House_(TV_series)" title="House (TV series)">House</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Bones_(TV_series)" title="Bones (TV series)">Bones</a></i>, and <i>Idol</i>, for years, had become Fox's strongest platform primetime television program for promoting eventual hit shows of the 2010s (of the same network) such as <i><a href="/wiki/Glee_(TV_series)" title="Glee (TV series)">Glee</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/New_Girl" title="New Girl">New Girl</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-carter_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carter-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The show, its creator Simon Fuller claimed, "saved Fox".<sup id="cite_ref-usatoday.com_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usatoday.com-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The show's massive success in the mid-2000s to early 2010s spawned a number of imitating singing-competition shows, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Rock_Star_(TV_series)" title="Rock Star (TV series)">Rock Star</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Nashville_Star" title="Nashville Star">Nashville Star</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Voice_(U.S._TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Voice (U.S. TV series)">The Voice</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Rising_Star_(TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Rising Star (TV series)">Rising Star</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sing-Off" title="The Sing-Off">The Sing-Off</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_X_Factor_(U.S.)" class="mw-redirect" title="The X Factor (U.S.)">The X Factor</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup>  The number of imitative singing shows on American television had reached 17 by its final year in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> Its format also served as a blueprint for non-singing TV shows such as <i><a href="/wiki/Dancing_with_the_Stars_(U.S._TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dancing with the Stars (U.S. TV series)">Dancing with the Stars</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/So_You_Think_You_Can_Dance_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="So You Think You Can Dance (United States)">So You Think You Can Dance</a></i>, most of which contribute to the current highly competitive reality TV landscape on American television.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_alumni_album_sales" class="mw-redirect" title="American Idol alumni album sales">American Idol alumni album sales</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_alumni_single_sales" class="mw-redirect" title="American Idol alumni single sales">American Idol alumni single sales</a></div>
<p>As one of the most successful shows on U.S. television history, <i>American Idol</i> had a strong impact not just on television, but also in the wider world of entertainment.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> It helped create a number of highly successful recording artists, such as Kelly Clarkson, Daughtry and Carrie Underwood, as well as others of varying notability. The alumni of the show had received between them 54 Grammy nominations and 13 Grammy awards by the end of the show in 2016, with Carrie Underwood winning seven.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Various <i>American Idol</i> alumni had success on various <a href="/wiki/Record_charts" class="mw-redirect" title="Record charts">record charts</a> around the world; in the U.S. they had achieved 345 number ones on the <i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">Billboard</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Billboard_charts" title="Billboard charts">charts</a> in its first 10 years,<sup id="cite_ref-Billboard2012_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard2012-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> and 458 by its last year of broadcast in 2016, with 100 achieved by Kelly Clarkson alone.<sup id="cite_ref-billboard_2_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-billboard_2-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Fred_Bronson" title="Fred Bronson">Fred Bronson</a>, author of books on the Billboard charts, no other entity has ever created as many hit-making artists and best-selling albums and singles.<sup id="cite_ref-Fred_Bronson_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fred_Bronson-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> In 2007, <i>American Idol</i> alums accounted for 2.1% of all music sales.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> Its alumni have a massive impact on radio; in 2007, <i>American Idol</i> had become "a dominant force in radio" according to the president of the research company <a href="/wiki/Mediabase" title="Mediabase">Mediabase</a> which monitors radio stations Rich Meyer.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> By 2010, four winners each had more than a million radio spins, with Kelly Clarkson leading the field with over four million spins.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the end of the show's run, Idol's contestants have sold more than 60 million albums in the US, resulting in more than 80 Platinum records and 95 Gold records. Its participants have generated more than 450 Billboard No. 1 hits and sold more than 260 million digital downloads.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film_and_theater">Film and theater</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Film and theater">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_alumni_in_film,_television_and_theater" title="American Idol alumni in film, television and theater">American Idol alumni in film, television and theater</a></div>
<p>The impact of <i>American Idol</i> was also strongly felt in musical theatre, where many of <i>Idol</i> alumni have forged successful careers. The striking effect of former <i>American Idol</i> contestants on Broadway has been noted and commented on.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> The casting of a popular <i>Idol</i> contestant can lead to significantly increased ticket sales. Other alumni have gone on to work in television and films, the most notable being Jennifer Hudson who, on the recommendation of the <i>Idol</i> vocal coach Debra Byrd,<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> won a role in <i><a href="/wiki/Dreamgirls_(film)" title="Dreamgirls (film)">Dreamgirls</a></i> and subsequently received an <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a> for her performance.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Revenue_and_commercial_ventures">Revenue and commercial ventures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Revenue and commercial ventures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:American_Idol_Experience.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/American_Idol_Experience.JPG/170px-American_Idol_Experience.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/American_Idol_Experience.JPG/255px-American_Idol_Experience.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/American_Idol_Experience.JPG/340px-American_Idol_Experience.JPG 2x" data-file-width="534" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:American_Idol_Experience.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The American Idol Experience</i> marquee sign</div></div></div>
<p>The dominance of <i>American Idol</i> in the ratings had made it the most profitable show in U.S. TV for many years. The show was estimated to generate $900&#160;million for the year 2004 through sales of TV ads, albums, merchandise and concert tickets.<sup id="cite_ref-usatoday.com_248-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usatoday.com-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> By the seventh season, the show was estimated to earn around $900&#160;million from its ad revenue alone, not including ancillary sponsorship deals and other income.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WSJ_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> One estimate puts the total TV revenue for the first eight seasons of American at $6.4&#160;billion.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> Sponsors that bought fully integrated packages can expect a variety of promotions of their products on the show, such as product placement, adverts and product promotion integrated into the show, and various promotional opportunities.<sup id="cite_ref-Billboard_May_12,_2009_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_May_12,_2009-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> Other off-air promotional partners pay for the rights to feature "Idol" branding on their packaging, products and marketing programs.<sup id="cite_ref-Brandweek_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brandweek-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> <i>American Idol</i> also partnered with Disney in its theme park attraction The American Idol Experience.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advertising_revenue">Advertising revenue</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Advertising revenue">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>American Idol</i> became the most expensive series on broadcast networks for advertisers starting the fourth season,<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> and by the next season, it had broken the record in advertising rate for a regularly scheduled prime-time network series, selling over $700,000 for a 30-seconds slot,<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> and reaching up to $1.3&#160;million for the finale.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> Its ad prices reached a peak in the seventh season at $737,000.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ_267-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> Estimated revenue more than doubled from $404&#160;million in the third season to $870&#160;million in the sixth season.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> While that declined from the eighth season onwards, it still earned significantly more than its nearest competitor,<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> with advertising revenue topping $800&#160;million annually the next few seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> However, the sharp drop in ratings in the eleventh season also resulted in a sharp drop in advertising rate for the twelfth season, and the show lost its leading position as the costliest show for advertisers.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup>  By 2014, ad revenue from had fallen to $427 million where a 30-second spot went for less than $300,000.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup>  For the relaunched <i>Idol</i> on ABC, it has been reported that a 30-second spot may cost between $120,000&#8211;$160,000.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_sponsorship">Media sponsorship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Media sponsorship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford Motor Company</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a> were two of the first sponsors of <i>American Idol</i> in its first season. The sponsorship deal cost around $10&#160;million in the first season,<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup> rising to $35&#160;million by the seventh season,<sup id="cite_ref-Brandweek_270-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brandweek-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> and between $50 to $60 million in the tenth season.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup> The third major sponsor AT&amp;T Wireless joined in the second season but ended after the twelfth season, and Coca-Cola officially ended its sponsorship after the thirteenth season amidst the declining ratings of <i>Idol</i> in the mid-2010s. <a href="/wiki/ITunes_Store" title="ITunes Store">iTunes</a> sponsored the show since the seventh season.
</p><p><i>American Idol</i> prominent display of its sponsors' logo and products had been noted since the early seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-Watching_TV:_six_decades_of_American_television_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watching_TV:_six_decades_of_American_television-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NY_Times_–_September_6,_2002_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NY_Times_–_September_6,_2002-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> By the sixth season, <i>Idol</i> showed 4,349 <a href="/wiki/Product_placement" title="Product placement">product placements</a> according to <a href="/wiki/Nielsen_Media_Research" title="Nielsen Media Research">Nielsen Media Research</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nielsen_2007_283-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nielsen_2007-283">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Branded_entertainment" class="mw-redirect" title="Branded entertainment">branded entertainment</a> integration proved beneficial to its advertisers – promotion of AT&amp;T text-messaging as a means to vote successfully introduced the technology into the wider culture,<sup id="cite_ref-ATT_2003_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATT_2003-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> and Coca-Cola has seen its equity increased during the show.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Coca-Cola – Cups bearing logo of Coca-Cola, and occasionally its subsidiary <a href="/wiki/Energy_Brands" title="Energy Brands">Vitaminwater</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup> are featured prominently on the judges table. Contestants are shown between songs held in the "Coca-Cola Red Room", the show's equivalent of the <a href="/wiki/Green_room" title="Green room">green room</a>. (The Coca-Cola logo however is obscured during rebroadcast in the UK which until 2011 banned product placement.<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup>)</li>
<li>Ford – Contestants appear in the special Ford videos on the results shows, and winners Kelly Clarkson, Taylor Hicks, and Kris Allen have also appeared in commercials for Ford.<sup id="cite_ref-Billboard_May_12,_2009_269-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_May_12,_2009-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> The final two each won a free <a href="/wiki/Ford_Mustang" title="Ford Mustang">Ford Mustang</a> in the fourth through sixth seasons, <a href="/wiki/Ford_Escape_Hybrid" class="mw-redirect" title="Ford Escape Hybrid">Ford Escape Hybrid</a> in the seventh season, <a href="/wiki/Ford_Fusion_Hybrid" title="Ford Fusion Hybrid">Ford Fusion Hybrid</a> in the eighth season, <a href="/wiki/Ford_Fiesta" title="Ford Fiesta">Ford Fiesta</a> in the ninth season, and 2013 <a href="/wiki/Ford_Fusion_(Americas)" title="Ford Fusion (Americas)">Ford Fusion</a> in the eleventh season. In the tenth season Scotty McCreery chose a <a href="/wiki/Ford_F-Series" title="Ford F-Series">Ford F-150</a> and Lauren Alaina chose <a href="/wiki/Shelby_Mustang" title="Shelby Mustang">Shelby Mustang</a>. In the red room, there is a glass table with a Ford wheel as its base.</li>
<li>AT&amp;T – <a href="/wiki/AT%26T_Mobility" title="AT&amp;T Mobility">AT&amp;T Mobility</a> is promoted as the service provider for text-voting. AT&amp;T created an ad campaign that centered on an air-headed teenager going around telling people to vote.</li>
<li>Apple iTunes – Ryan Seacrest announces the availability of contestants' performances exclusively via iTunes. Videos are regularly shown of contestants learning their songs by rehearsing with iPods.</li>
<li>Previous sponsors include <a href="/wiki/Old_Navy" title="Old Navy">Old Navy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clairol" title="Clairol">Clairol</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Herbal_Essences" title="Herbal Essences">Herbal Essences</a>. In the second and third seasons, contestants sometimes donned Old Navy clothing for their performances with celebrity stylist <a href="/wiki/Steven_Cojocaru" title="Steven Cojocaru">Steven Cojocaru</a> assisting with their wardrobe selection,<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> and contestants received Clairol-guided hair makeovers. In the seventh-season finale, both David Cook and David Archuleta appeared in "<a href="/wiki/Risky_Business" title="Risky Business">Risky Business</a>"-inspired commercials for <a href="/wiki/Guitar_Hero" title="Guitar Hero">Guitar Hero</a>, a sponsor of the tour that year.</li></ul>
<p>Coca-Cola's archrival <a href="/wiki/PepsiCo" title="PepsiCo">PepsiCo</a> declined to sponsor <i>American Idol</i> at the show's start. What the <i>Los Angeles Times</i> later called "missing one of the biggest marketing opportunities in a generation" contributed to <a href="/wiki/Pepsi" title="Pepsi">Pepsi</a> losing market share, by 2010 falling to third place from second in the United States. PepsiCo sponsored the American version of Cowell's <i>The X Factor</i> in hopes of not repeating its <i>Idol</i> mistake until its cancellation.<sup id="cite_ref-james20110317_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-james20110317-289">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For the revived series on ABC, <a href="/wiki/Macy%27s" title="Macy&#39;s">Macy's</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johnson_%26_Johnson" title="Johnson &amp; Johnson">Johnson &amp; Johnson</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Zyrtec" class="mw-redirect" title="Zyrtec">Zyrtec</a> signed on as the major sponsors of the show.<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="American_Idol_tour">American Idol tour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: American Idol tour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:American_Idol_Tour_Season_10_Scotty_McCreery.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/American_Idol_Tour_Season_10_Scotty_McCreery.jpg/220px-American_Idol_Tour_Season_10_Scotty_McCreery.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/American_Idol_Tour_Season_10_Scotty_McCreery.jpg/330px-American_Idol_Tour_Season_10_Scotty_McCreery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/American_Idol_Tour_Season_10_Scotty_McCreery.jpg/440px-American_Idol_Tour_Season_10_Scotty_McCreery.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1280" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:American_Idol_Tour_Season_10_Scotty_McCreery.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tenth season American Idol tour, Scotty McCreery performing with Thia Megia, Haley Reinhart and Pia Toscano</div></div></div>
<p>The top ten (eleven in the tenth season, five in the fourteenth season, and seven in the sixteenth season) toured at the end of every season except for the fifteenth and seventeenth seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/American_Idols_LIVE!_Tour_2013" class="mw-redirect" title="American Idols LIVE! Tour 2013">twelfth season tour</a> a semi-finalist who won a sing-off was also added to the tour. <a href="/wiki/Kellogg_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Kellogg Company">Kellogg</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Pop-Tarts" title="Pop-Tarts">Pop-Tarts</a> was the sponsor for the first seven seasons, and Guitar Hero was added for the <a href="/wiki/American_Idols_LIVE!_Tour_2008" class="mw-redirect" title="American Idols LIVE! Tour 2008">seventh season tour</a>. <a href="/wiki/M%26M%27s" title="M&amp;M&#39;s">M&amp;M's</a> Pretzel Chocolate Candies was a sponsor of the <a href="/wiki/American_Idols_LIVE!_Tour_2010" class="mw-redirect" title="American Idols LIVE! Tour 2010">ninth season tour</a>. The <a href="/wiki/American_Idols_LIVE!_Tour_2006" class="mw-redirect" title="American Idols LIVE! Tour 2006">fifth season tour</a> was the most successful tour with gross of over $35&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup>  However no concert tour was organized in the fifteenth and seventeenth seasons, the only seasons not to have an associated tour.<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_Live!_2018" title="American Idol Live! 2018">sixteenth season tour</a> featured the band <a href="/wiki/In_Real_Life_(band)" title="In Real Life (band)">In Real Life</a> as an opener on select dates.<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Idol_Gives_Back"><i>Idol Gives Back</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Idol Gives Back">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Idol_Gives_Back" title="Idol Gives Back">Idol Gives Back</a></div>
<p><i>Idol Gives Back</i> was a special charity event started in season six featuring performances by celebrities and various fund-raising initiatives. This event was also held in seasons seven and nine and has raised nearly $185&#160;million in total.<sup id="cite_ref-idol_gives_back_80-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-idol_gives_back-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music_releases">Music releases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Music releases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_compilation_series" title="American Idol compilation series">American Idol compilation series</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_Hot_100_singles" class="mw-redirect" title="American Idol Hot 100 singles">American Idol Hot 100 singles</a></div>
<p><i>American Idol</i> has traditionally released studio recordings of contestants' performances as well as the winner's coronation single for sale. For the first five seasons, the recordings were released as a compilation album at the end of the season. All five of these albums reached the top ten in Billboard 200 which made then <i>American Idol</i> the most successful soundtrack franchise of any motion picture or television program.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup> Starting late in the fifth season, individual performances were released during the season as digital downloads, initially from the <i>American Idol</i> official website only. In the seventh season the live performances and studio recordings were made available during the season from iTunes when it joined as a sponsor. In the tenth season the weekly studio recordings were also released as compilation digital album straight after performance night.
</p><p>19 Recordings, a recording label owned by 19 Entertainment, currently hold the rights to phonographic material recorded by all the contestants. 19 originally partnered with <a href="/wiki/Bertelsmann_Music_Group" title="Bertelsmann Music Group">Bertelsmann Music Group</a> (BMG) to promote and distribute the recordings through its labels <a href="/wiki/RCA_Records" title="RCA Records">RCA Records</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arista_Records" title="Arista Records">Arista Records</a>, <a href="/wiki/J_Records" title="J Records">J Records</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jive_Records" title="Jive Records">Jive Records</a>. In 2005–2007, BMG partnered with <a href="/wiki/Sony_Music_Entertainment" class="mw-redirect" title="Sony Music Entertainment">Sony Music Entertainment</a> to form a joint venture known as <a href="/wiki/Sony_BMG_Music_Entertainment" class="mw-redirect" title="Sony BMG Music Entertainment">Sony BMG Music Entertainment</a>. From 2008 to 2010, Sony Music handled the distribution following their acquisition of BMG. <a href="/wiki/Sony_Music_Entertainment" class="mw-redirect" title="Sony Music Entertainment">Sony Music</a> was partnered with <i>American Idol</i> and distribute its music, and In 2010, Sony was replaced by as the music label for <i>American Idol</i> by <a href="/wiki/Universal_Music_Group" title="Universal Music Group">UMG</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Interscope-Geffen-A%26M" class="mw-redirect" title="Interscope-Geffen-A&amp;M">Interscope-Geffen-A&amp;M</a> Records.<sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tie-ins">Tie-ins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Tie-ins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b><i>American Idol</i> video games</b>
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/American_Idol_(video_game)" title="American Idol (video game)">American Idol</a></i> – <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_2" title="PlayStation 2">PlayStation 2</a>, <a href="/wiki/Personal_Computer" class="mw-redirect" title="Personal Computer">PC</a>, <a href="/wiki/Game_Boy_Advance" title="Game Boy Advance">Game Boy Advance</a>, mobile phone</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Karaoke_Revolution" title="Karaoke Revolution">Karaoke Revolution Presents American Idol</a></i> – PlayStation 2</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Karaoke_Revolution" title="Karaoke Revolution">Karaoke Revolution Presents American Idol Encore</a></i> – PlayStation 2, <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_3" title="PlayStation 3">PlayStation 3</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wii" title="Wii">Wii</a>, Xbox 360</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Karaoke_Revolution" title="Karaoke Revolution">Karaoke Revolution Presents American Idol Encore 2</a></i> – PlayStation 3, <a href="/wiki/Wii" title="Wii">Wii</a>, Xbox 360</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theme_park_attraction">Theme park attraction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Theme park attraction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_American_Idol_Experience" title="The American Idol Experience">The American Idol Experience</a></div>
<p>On February 14, 2009, <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Company" title="The Walt Disney Company">The Walt Disney Company</a> debuted "<a href="/wiki/The_American_Idol_Experience" title="The American Idol Experience">The American Idol Experience</a>" at its <a href="/wiki/Disney%27s_Hollywood_Studios" title="Disney&#39;s Hollywood Studios">Disney's Hollywood Studios</a> theme park at the <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_World_Resort" class="mw-redirect" title="Walt Disney World Resort">Walt Disney World Resort</a> in Florida. In this live production, co-produced by 19 Entertainment, park guests chose from a list of songs and auditioned privately for Disney cast members. Those selected then performed on a stage in a 1000-seat theater replicating the <i>Idol</i> set. Three judges, whose mannerisms and style mimicked those of the real <i>Idol</i> judges, critiqued the performances.<sup id="cite_ref-AI_experience_298-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_experience-298">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> Audience members then voted for their favorite performer. There were several preliminary-round shows during the day that culminated in a "finals" show in the evening where one of the winners of the previous rounds that day was selected as the overall winner.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> The winner of the finals show received a "Dream Ticket" that granted them front-of-the-line privileges at any future <i>American Idol</i> audition.<sup id="cite_ref-AI_experience_298-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_experience-298">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> The attraction closed on August 30, 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_broadcasts">Other broadcasts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Idol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Other broadcasts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>American Idol</i> is broadcast to over 100 nations worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> In most nations these are not live broadcasts and may be tape delayed by several days or weeks, except for other season finales that are aired live in several countries simulcast with the U.S. broadcast on Fox (2002–2016) and ABC (since 2018). In Canada, the first thirteen seasons of American Idol were aired live countrywide by <a href="/wiki/CTV_Television_Network" title="CTV Television Network">CTV</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/CTV_Two" class="mw-redirect" title="CTV Two">CTV Two</a>, in simulcast with Fox. CTV dropped <i>Idol</i> after its thirteenth season and in August 2014, <a href="/wiki/Yes_TV" title="Yes TV">Yes TV</a> announced that it had picked up Canadian rights to <i>American Idol</i> beginning in its 2015 season.<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cts-sayyes_303-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cts-sayyes-303">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup> In 2017, it was announced the show would return to <a href="/wiki/CTV_Two" class="mw-redirect" title="CTV Two">CTV Two</a> for its sixteenth season.<sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Latin America, the show is broadcast and subtitled by <a href="/wiki/Sony_Entertainment_Television_(Latin_America)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sony Entertainment Television (Latin America)">Sony Entertainment Television</a>. In Southeast Asia, it is broadcast by <a href="/wiki/Sony_Channel_(Southeast_Asia)" title="Sony Channel (Southeast Asia)">Sony Channel</a> every Monday and Tuesday nine or ten hours after since its revival. In Philippines, it is aired every Thursday and Friday nine or ten hours after its United States telecast; from 2004 to 2007 on <a href="/wiki/The_5_Network" class="mw-redirect" title="The 5 Network">ABC 5</a>; 2008–11 on <a href="/wiki/Q_(TV_network)" title="Q (TV network)">QTV</a>, then <a href="/wiki/GMA_News_TV" title="GMA News TV">GMA News TV</a>; and since 2012 on <a href="/wiki/ETC_(Philippine_TV_network)" title="ETC (Philippine TV network)">ETC</a>. In Australia, it aired a few hours after the U.S. telecast. It was aired on <a href="/wiki/Network_Ten" class="mw-redirect" title="Network Ten">Network Ten</a> from 2002 to 2008 and then again in 2013. Between 2008 and 2012 it aired on <a href="/wiki/Fox8" title="Fox8">Fox8</a> and the thirteenth and fourteenth seasons (2014–15) it aired on digital channel, <a href="/wiki/Eleven_(TV_Channel)" class="mw-redirect" title="Eleven (TV Channel)">Eleven</a>, a sister channel to Network Ten. Its final season (2016) aired on Fox8 hours after the original U.S. broadcast. The show enjoyed a lot of popularity in Australia throughout the 2000s before declining in the ratings.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup> In the United Kingdom, episodes were aired one day after the U.S. broadcast on digital channel <a href="/wiki/ITV2" title="ITV2">ITV2</a>. In the twelfth season, the episodes aired on <a href="/wiki/5*" class="mw-redirect" title="5*">5*</a>. It was also aired in Ireland on <a href="/wiki/TV3_(Ireland)" class="mw-redirect" title="TV3 (Ireland)">TV3</a> two days after the telecast. In Brazil and Israel, the show airs two days after its original broadcast, and broadcast live during the season finale. In the instances where the airing is delayed, the shows may sometimes be combined into one episode to summarize the results. In Italy, the twelfth season was broadcast by La3.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup> In Singapore, The show was broadcast on <a href="/wiki/Channel_5_(Singapore)" title="Channel 5 (Singapore)">Mediacorp Channel 5</a> for the <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_(season_9)" title="American Idol (season 9)">ninth</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_Idol_(season_10)" title="American Idol (season 10)">tenth</a> seasons.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-137"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-137">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>American Idol</i> does not have a ranking for the 2001–2002 season because it aired in the summer of 2002. If it had aired within the official 2001–2002 U.S. television season, the Wednesday results show would have ranked number 25 and the Tuesday performance show would have ranked number 30, assuming it would have had the same rating as it did in the summer.</span>
